                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/cssc.202301223')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7123e9f7d229476abc6133e44248c18d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3e2b5616-56e6-4e6f-af14-d52e8893fb56'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

  document.getElementById("journal-banner-text").style.fontSize = "22px";
  document.getElementById("journal-banner-image").style.display = "none";

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d156382214" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A simple condensation polymerization method is adopted to successfully prepare polyimide composite (PI/CNT) anodes with stable structure, high storage capacity and fast reaction kinetics, which are conducive to improve electrochemical properties and cycling stability of the constructed dual-ion battery.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4975ff0d-8dac-4abd-a53d-a66a2707bff6/cssc202301223-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4975ff0d-8dac-4abd-a53d-a66a2707bff6/cssc202301223-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/27e6054f-635c-461d-a82d-88d8faa7548b/cssc202301223-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4975ff0d-8dac-4abd-a53d-a66a2707bff6/cssc202301223-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d156382217" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Considering the extensive resources, flexible structural designability, and abundant active sites, organic electrodes have been considered as the ideal sodium storage materials. However, organic materials generally face the limitations of unstable and dissolved characteristic, leading to a poor cyclic stability. In this work, we proposed a carbon nanotube (CNT) modified polyimide as the anode for sodium-based dual-ion battery (SDIB). The polyimide remains well the structure and morphology of monomer with a stable conjugated structure and high degree of crystallinity, effectively enhancing the electrochemical performance of the SDIBs. Also, the cooperation with CNT particularly improves the ion conductivity of the anode and advances the rate performance. Combined with an ionic liquid electrolyte, the constructed dual-ion battery exhibits excellent rate capability, high specific discharge capacity and stable cycling performance. It delivers a specific discharge capacity of 119.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C (1 C=100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and still has a specific discharge capacity of 82.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even after 1000 cycles at 10 C. Besides, the system displays a low self-discharge rate and stable fast charging performance, which is expected to be applied in the large-scale electrochemical energy storage devices and inspire the future development of SDIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202301223-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapid growth of the global economy has led to an increasing demand for energy, which in turn accelerates the massive extraction and utilization of non-renewable fossil energy. Although the consumption of coal, oil and natural gas promotes the development of society, it also creates serious environmental problems, such as the greenhouse effect, haze and water pollution. Therefore, it is imperative to develop the extensive energy resources and efficient energy storage devices to meet the great demand.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0001_R_d156382206e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">1</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0002_R_d156382206e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy">2</a></sup></span> At present, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are the state-of-the-art electrochemical storage systems, which have been widely employed in portable electronic devices such as mobile phones and laptops due to the high energy density, superior cyclic stability, and no memory effect.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0003_R_d156382206e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0004_R_d156382206e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Rare Met.">4</a></sup></span> However, the limited and uneven distribution of lithium resources have led to the increasing cost of LIBs. In addition, the organic carbonate solvents commonly used in LIBs are flammable and prone to decomposition at high operating potential, degrading the electrochemical performance and even causing safety hazards.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0005_R_d156382206e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy">5</a></sup></span> Accordingly, it is urgent to develop the novel low-cost and high-performance energy storage technologies to satisfy the market demand.</p>
         
         <p>In this context, the exploitation of innovative alternative energy storage system has become a priority for the development of electrochemical energy storage devices. The DIB is an emerging type of battery developed in recent years that has caught widespread concern as a promising option.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0006_R_d156382206e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0007_R_d156382206e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano-Micro Lett.">7</a></sup></span> Compared with the traditional LIBs, DIBs show higher operating voltage, lower cost and improved safety, more in line with the concept of green batteries.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0008_R_d156382206e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Battery Energy">8</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0009_R_d156382206e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun. (Camb.)">9</a></sup></span> Moreover, sodium exhibits similar electrochemical properties to lithium, and possesses more abundant resources compared with lithium, which makes it possible to build an efficient and lost-cost sodium-based dual-ion battery energy storage system for large-scale energy storage applications. Generally, the anodes of SDIBs are classified into intercalation materials, alloying materials, conversion materials and organic materials. Graphite is the most typical intercalation material. Previous researches have demonstrated that graphite is characterized by highly stable, low-cost and non-polluting, which enables reversible ion intercalation/deintercalation. Based on these merits, graphite is the most common electrode active material for DIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0010_R_d156382206e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0011_R_d156382206e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span> However, as an anode material, graphite provides a limited capacity and shows worse reaction kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0012_R_d156382206e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></sup></span> Moreover, the alloy-type materials based on alloying reactions are also usually considered for the anode materials of DIBs owing to their superior capability of storing metal cations and high theoretical capacity. For example, Sheng et al. first proposed a novel tin-graphite dual-ion battery based on a sodium-ion containing electrolyte, which exhibited higher energy density.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0013_R_d156382206e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span> Conversion materials refer to some transition metal oxides and sulfides based on conversion reactions. Tang et al. synthesized the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanocomposites as anodes for MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIBs, which displayed a specific discharge capacity of 65 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C and excellent cyclic stability after 200 cycles.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0014_R_d156382206e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a></sup></span> However, alloying materials and conversion materials undergo severe volume expansion and pulverization during charging and discharging process, resulting in poor electrochemical performance. Furthermore, the high cost and non-renewability of these materials do not fit “green energy storage concept”, which hinders the sustainability of electrochemistry.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0015_R_d156382206e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Chem. Rev.">15</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Organic electrode materials normally achieve electrochemical reactions based on the charge conversion of active functional groups. Given their structural flexibility, the energy density and power density of the battery combined with the modulation of the molecular structure could be effortlessly optimized.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0016_R_d156382206e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small">16</a>-<a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0018_R_d156382206e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span> Moreover, organic materials are rich in considerable natural resources. Therefore, organic materials are born with inherent advantages that are unmatched by inorganic materials, which have drawn wide attention from researchers.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0019_R_d156382206e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0021_R_d156382206e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">21</a></sup></span> In particular, conjugated carbonyl compounds are the most extensively researched organic electrode materials because of their high storage capacity, fast reaction dynamics, structural diversity and easy adjustment.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0022_R_d156382206e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a>-<a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0025_R_d156382206e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a></sup></span> Yet, it is well known that organic small-molecule monomer materials have limitations such as structural instability, easy decomposition and aggregation. Hence, a feasible approach to avoid the decomposition is to construct a polymer with a stabilized inactive framework and highly electroactive functional groups.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0026_R_d156382206e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0027_R_d156382206e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span> A few polymers with redox activity have been reported for electrochemical energy storage, but the exhibited low specific discharge capacity and the safety hazards associated with their sophisticated preparation processes limit the further practical applications of these polymers. In this regard, polyimides are the favorable choice due to their small molar mass per unit of monomer and convenience of preparation.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0028_R_d156382206e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span> Polyimide is similar to dianhydride in that its aromatic imide groups are equally capable of reversible electrochemical reduction and oxidation.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0029_R_d156382206e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J.">29</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0030_R_d156382206e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Electrochim. Acta">30</a></sup></span> By utilizing the conjugation effect, stable enolates could be formed.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0031_R_d156382206e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span> and its conjugated structure will facilitate the insertion of Na+ ions, improving the electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0032_R_d156382206e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ChemPhysChem">32</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0033_R_d156382206e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mol. Liq.">33</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>On the other hand, owing to the inherent insulating properties of polyimide render poor ion conductivity, its reaction kinetics is very slow in practical applications. The rate performance and capacity retention after long-term cycling are not optimistic either. To solve these problems, researchers usually introduce the high electrical conductivity carbon nanomaterials, such as carbon nanotubes, graphene, etc.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0034_R_d156382206e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">34</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0035_R_d156382206e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Composites Part B">35</a></sup></span> The carbon-nanotubes (CNTs) possess a one-dimensional structure, high electrical conductivity and excellent mechanical properties, which could facilitate the transfer of electrons and expand the available active sites.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0036_R_d156382206e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span> Therefore, the doping of CNTs is expected to improve the rate performance and cycling stability of polyimide in this work.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report a strategy to design a carbon nanotube modified polyimide composite anode material for the sodium dual-ion batteries to achieve excellent rate capability and prolonged cycle life. Polyimide/carbon nanotube (PI/CNT) nanocomposites were successfully prepared by the in-situ polymerization. Such polymerization method can further improve the cycling stability of electrode materials for DIBs. In addition, given that the organic electrolytes are flammable and easily decomposed. Therefore, ionic liquid containing sodium salt is used as an electrolyte in this work. Ionic liquids have drawn great interest for high-performance electrolyte due to the excellent stability, nonflammable and environmentally friendly. Among the various ionic liquids, the room temperature ionic liquid Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI shows wide electrochemical window, high stability and considerable conductivity. Therefore, this ionic liquid containing sodium salt is chosen as an electrolyte, which could broaden the electrolyte electrochemical window, enhance the stability of electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0037_R_d156382206e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></sup></span> The constructed SDIB exhibits remarkably excellent electrochemical performance. Furthermore, the working mechanism of the SDIB and the structural stability of the electrodes are demonstrated by a series of characterization and measurements.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202301223-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202301223-sec-0003-title"> Physical and chemical property characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The polyimides were synthesized by a simple condensation method, and the detailed synthesis equation is described in Scheme S1. Firstly, the morphologies of PTCDA, PI and PI/CNT were observed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As shown in Figure S1, the organic monomer PTCDA exhibits a rod-like structure. Obviously, the polymer PI also forms a nanorod shape, which retains the morphologic structure of the PTCDA monomer (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0001">1a</a>–<a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0001">b</a>). The composites with CNTs are displayed in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0001">1c</a>–<a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0001">d</a>, from which it is clear that the CNTs are interwoven and randomly dispersed between the PI nanorods. Part of the CNTs exhibit agglomeration phenomenon as it is hardly to disperse the CNTs uniformly even by ultrasonic treatment.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0038_R_d156382206e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Microporous Mesoporous Mater.">38</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the elemental mapping based on the Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0001">1e</a>–<a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0001">h</a> clearly shows the uniform distribution of C, N and O elements. Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0002">2a</a> demonstrates the XRD patterns of the organic monomer PTCDA, PI and PI/CNT materials. The diffraction peaks of PTCDA at 8.8°, 12.4°, 25.1°, 27.4° and 27.7° correspond to the (011), (021), (042), (102) and (112) crystal planes of PTCDA, respectively, showing a favorable crystallinity.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0039_R_d156382206e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a></sup></span> Compared with the XRD pattern of PTCDA, the polymers maintain the typical diffraction of PTCDA, indicating that the polymer maintains the crystal structures of the PTCDA, which facilitates the fast transport of ions.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0040_R_d156382206e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">40</a></sup></span> The reflection peak at around 2θ=12.4° is attributed to the ordered stacking of perylene cores along the N−N direction. Moreover, PI has a strong crystal peak at 27.5°, which corresponds to the characteristic intermolecular π-π stacking in the polymer structure. The strong intermolecular π-π interactions could be beneficial for facilitating the charge transfer rate and enhancing the rate performance of PI.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0041_R_d156382206e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Soft Matter">41</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, there are no obvious diffraction peaks attributed to CNTs, which can be ascribed to the weak peaks of the low-crystalline CNTs covered by the high-crystalline PI. The FT-IR spectrum of PTCDA exhibits broad stretching vibrational bands at 1770 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1730 cm<sup>−1</sup>, while the two sharp peaks of PI are showed at 1701 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1690 cm<sup>−1</sup>, originating from the carbonyl (C=O) asymmetry and the stretching frequency in imide groups (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0002">2b</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0031_R_d156382206e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span> A broadened band observed for the polymer at 1592 cm<sup>−1</sup> belongs to the stretching frequency of the aromatic C=C (perylene C=C), reflecting the complete retention of the functional group of the monomer PTCDA in the polymer. The vibrational bands at 1360 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1362 cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to the stretching frequencies of the imide C−N bonds in PI and PI/CNT, separately. The above-mentioned results indicate that the anhydride functionality is entirely converted into the imide functionality during the polymerization reaction.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0040_R_d156382206e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0042_R_d156382206e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">42</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/145f3744-0eec-40a3-9e9f-840f2b2beb4d/cssc202301223-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/145f3744-0eec-40a3-9e9f-840f2b2beb4d/cssc202301223-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/998aaa15-723e-4710-9161-5250eb0dabd1/cssc202301223-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/145f3744-0eec-40a3-9e9f-840f2b2beb4d/cssc202301223-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>SEM images of PI and PI/CNT: (a, b) PI at low and high magnification. (c, d) PI/CNT at low and high magnification. (e–h) Elemental analysis.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b1890a1b-3e2b-4b24-aba7-7b5ea3701798/cssc202301223-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b1890a1b-3e2b-4b24-aba7-7b5ea3701798/cssc202301223-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e83bf47a-758d-4f6c-adb8-f85d44fd81f9/cssc202301223-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b1890a1b-3e2b-4b24-aba7-7b5ea3701798/cssc202301223-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Characterizations of the CNT, PTCDA, PI and PI/CNT: (a) XRD. (b) FT-IR. (c) TGA. (d–f) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption curves and pore size distribution.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0002">2c</a> shows the TG curves of the PTCDA and PI/CNT. Compared to the pyrolysis temperature of PTCDA (302 °C), the PI/CNT composites possess a higher pyrolysis temperature. The PI/CNT composites keep a stability weight loss before 516 °C and decompose quickly from 516 °C to 800 °C. The decrease of the curve in the 0–516 °C could be attributed to the volatilization of the intramolecular solvent and residual solvent, with a weight loss of about 5 %. On the other hand, the rapid falling curve of the 516–799 °C phase is the result of the high temperature decomposition of the material. When the temperature rises to 516 °C, the imide ring in the polyimide structure undergoes a cracking reaction, resulting in a loss of mass as well as a drastic decrease in thermal stability. The results of the TG diagram demonstrate the excellent thermal stability of PI/CNT. Besides, by observing the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm of PI/CNT (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0002">2d</a>), the specific surface area of the material is 29.72 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> smaller than that of the monomer (46.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>). It may be due to the exceptionally small BET of CNT (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0002">2e</a>), which makes the BET of the polyimide lower after CNT doping. Apart from it, PI/CNT is primarily presented in mesoporous structure with pore size distribution of 2–50 nm, which is more favorable to improve the transport kinetics of active ions and enhance the multiplicative performance (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0002">2f</a>). Moreover, the PI/CNT composites were also charactered by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0003">3</a>). As depicted in the XPS full spectrum (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0003">3a</a>), three sharp peaks at 284.8 eV, 400.4 eV and 532.1 eV could be corresponded to elements C 1s, N 1s and O 1s, respectively. In the high-resolution C 1s energy level spectrogram (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0003">3b</a>), the peak with a binding energy of 284.6 eV is attributed to the C−C bond, and the weak signal peaks at 285.5 eV and 289.2 eV can be assigned to the C=O and N-C=O bonds, indicating the successful introduction of the amide groups.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0043_R_d156382206e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Eng. J.">43</a></sup></span> The O 1s spectrogram (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0003">3c</a>) is resolved into two peaks at 532.0 eV and 533.4 eV, which can be attributed to the C=O bond. The signal peak at 400.4 eV in the high-resolution N 1s spectrogram (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0003">3d</a>) represents the N-C=O bond, reflecting the characteristic imide functional groups in polyimide. The above XPS spectrum analysis is in agreement with the results of FT-IR.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b3964d2a-eb0d-4cf0-9f74-b892a58579f1/cssc202301223-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b3964d2a-eb0d-4cf0-9f74-b892a58579f1/cssc202301223-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b072db75-9ca8-45ad-912e-d71b6bdf9c88/cssc202301223-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b3964d2a-eb0d-4cf0-9f74-b892a58579f1/cssc202301223-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>XPS characterization of PI/CNT: (a) Full spectrum. (b) High resolution C 1s spectrum. (c) High resolution O 1s spectrum. (d) High resolution N 1s spectrum.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202301223-sec-0004-title"> Electrochemical characterization of PI/CNT as DIB anode</h3>
            
            <p>Figure S2 presents the oxidative stability measurements of the 1 M NaTFSI-Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI electrolyte and 1 M NaTFSI-EC-DEC electrolyte obtained by linear sweep voltammetry. Obviously, the organic electrolyte decomposes at 2.89 V, while the ionic liquid electrolyte decomposes only at 5.49 V, suggesting that ionic liquid is effective in broadening the electrolyte electrochemical window. The electrochemical performances of the batteries with CNT, PTCDA, PI and PI/CNT anode (graphite as cathode) were conducted on CR2025 coin batteries, and named CNT-DIB, PTCDA-DIB, PI-DIB and PI/CNT-DIB, respectively. The electrochemical behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> in CNTs, PTCDA and two types of polymers (PI, PI/CNT) along with the electrochemical performance of the batteries were investigated via the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD), cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0004">4a</a> reveals the rate performance of the PTCDA-DIB, PI-DIB and PI/CNT-DIB. As for the PI/CNT-DIB, the specific discharge capacities at 0.2 C, 0.5 C, 2 C, 5 C, 10 C, 20 C and 30 C are 119.3, 103.9, 86.7, 79.8, 75.3, 71.6 and 69.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The corresponding GCD curves show in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0004">4b</a>. As the current density increases, the corresponding specific discharge capacities gradually decrease owing to the strengthening of electrochemical polarization. Notably, after the current raising to a high rate of 30 C, the specific discharge capacity still reaches 69.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting that the PI/CNT anode researched in this work is able to withstand cycling at a higher current density. When the rate was restored to 0.5 C, the specific discharge capacity reaches 109.2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than the initial value, reflecting the fairly decent rate performance and stability of PI/CNT that in contrast to the sluggish kinetics when reactive ions insert into the inorganic materials. By comparison, the rate performance of PI-DIB shows a dramatic decrease in capacity after the current density increases to 5 C, followed by a sudden drop in specific discharge capacity to about 10 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density continues to increase to 30 C. Such phenomenon is attributed to the low conductivity of polyimide itself, which fails to respond to the rapid change of current density. The specific discharge capacity of PTCDA-DIB is close to 0 when the current density increases to 5 C, suggesting the great advantages resulted from the polymerization strategy and the introduction of CNT.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bb314063-9a2c-4a55-8ce0-a07e6e7ebe02/cssc202301223-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bb314063-9a2c-4a55-8ce0-a07e6e7ebe02/cssc202301223-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e2c84793-2c57-46ba-8d6f-3f46a6ef28df/cssc202301223-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bb314063-9a2c-4a55-8ce0-a07e6e7ebe02/cssc202301223-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance of SDIBs. (a) Rate performance and (b) GCD curves of PI/CNT-DIB at different rates. (c) Cyclic performance at 1 C and (d) Corresponding GCD curves of PI/CNT-DIB under different cycle numbers. (e) The long-term cycling performance under 10 C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The batteries with the as-prepared CNTs, PTCDA, PI and PI/CNT electrode were tested by GCD cycling at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. As evidenced by the cyclic performance in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0004">4c</a>, the CNTs selected in this work exhibit an extremely low specific discharge capacity of 2 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 70 cycles. Since only 5 % of the CNTs are compounded with polyimide in this scheme, it can be concluded that the CNTs hardly contribute to the capacity of the batteries, so the majority capacity of the battery with PI/CNT anode derives from the polyimide. Obviously, the specific discharge capacity of the PTCDA-DIB increased from 20 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> to 45 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 70 cycles, revealing an electrochemical activation process. Compared with the PTCDA-DIB, both of PI-DIB and PI/CNT-DIB show higher specific discharge capacity, which could be attributed to the contribution of the conjugated structure originated from the polymer design strategy. Furthermore, benefitting from the introduction of the CNT, a considerably improvement in the specific discharge capacity of PI/CNT-DIB is achieved, increasing to 106.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (74.6 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> of PI-DIB) due to the unique electronic structure of CNTs and their outstanding properties improve the conductivity and stabilization of the electrode active material.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0044_R_d156382206e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">44</a></sup></span> The capacity retention close to 100 % and the coulombic efficiency is maintained at about 99.8 %, reflecting the superior cycling stability. Besides, the cycle stability of the dual-ion battery is further enhanced by the addition of CNTs, leading to faster stabilization and shortening the activation time required for the anode active material. The variety of GCD curves of the PI/CNT-DIB at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are depicted in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0004">4d</a>. It can be clearly seen that two pair of charge-discharge plateaus located around 2.0 V and 2.3 V, respectively, which can be ascribe to the insertion/de-insertion of TFSI<sup>−</sup> ion and the two-step redox reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions with the carbonyl covalent bond in the PI/CNT. The specific charge capacity of the first cycle is 172 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, and the specific discharge capacity reaches a value of 71 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, with a low coulombic efficiency of only 49.9 %. This is probably related to the formation of SEI film on the electrode surface and the activation process of the active material.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0045_R_d156382206e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">45</a></sup></span> The curves at the 40th, 50th, 60th and 70th cycles are highly overlapping and the discharge plateau tends to be stable, revealing the excellent cycling stability and favorable reversibility of the PI/CNT-DIB. It is also can be confirmed by Figure S3, and the middle discharge plateaus keeps at 1.84 V without any fluctuation during the charge-discharge cycles. In addition, the PI/CNT anode also delivers outstanding cycling performance at a high rate. The CNT-DIB, PTCDA-DIB, PI-DIB and PI/CNT-DIB are individually cycled for 1000 cycles at a high rate of 10 C. The long cycle performance comparison in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0004">4e</a> shows that the specific discharge capacity of the PI/CNT-DIB anode is significantly improved compared with the PTCDA-DIB, PI-DIB with no capacity decay, and the specific discharge capacity of 82.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> could still be maintained for 1000 cycles. And the coulombic efficiency is greater than 99 % and the capacity retention rate is close to 100 %. Thus, it is clear that PI/CNT-DIB features rapid ion diffusion dynamics and remarkable cycling stability. In contrast, due to the lower electrical conductivity of polyimide, the long cycling performance exhibited by PI-DIB is far inferior to that of PI/CNT-DIB, showing an obvious capacity fluctuation and lack of stability during the cycling process. Also, we have compared the electrochemical properties of PI/CNT composite anode with other composite anode materials in terms of specific discharge capacity and cycling stability exhibited during charging and discharging, revealing the superior performance of the designed DIBs (see Table S1).</p>
            
            <p>Subsequently, the electrochemical behavior of the PI/CNT-DIB are analyzed by cyclic voltammetry (CV), and the battery is scanned from the open-circuit-voltage (OCV) in the negative direction at a voltage range of −3.8 V to −0.01 V with a rate of 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The results are shown in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0005">5a</a>. From the first cycle, it can be clearly observed that the peak current response is strengthened from −2.0 V and the reduction peaks appear at −2.4 V and −2.8 V, indicating the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the PI/CNT structure. The oxidation peaks are located at −2.1 V and −1.48 V, revealing that the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from the PI/CNT, corresponding to the two defined discharge plateaus in the curves of Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0004">4d</a>, respectively. The CV curves in the subsequent cycles are shifted to the right, which is due to the formation of the SEI film during the first cycle and the irreversible reactions such as the decomposition of the electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0046_R_d156382206e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Environ. Sci.">46</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0047_R_d156382206e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nat. Commun.">47</a></sup></span> The CV curves for the first five cycles show the very distinct and sharp peaks, and the CV curves overlap quite well in the last four cycles, indicating the outstanding reversibility of the insertion/de-insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into/from the PI/CNT material. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) test is conducted on the full battery to investigate the reaction kinetics. The semicircle in the high frequency region represents the charge transfer resistance (Rct). As viewed in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0005">5b</a>, with the increase of cycling number, the Rct value of 30.5 Ω for the 10th cycle exhibits less variation, indicating the formation of a stable SEI film and accelerating active ion transport. Moreover, its slope line representing the Weber impedance in the low frequency region shows a very high slope, indicating excellent ion diffusion kinetics.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/490e65c0-c9da-43f0-85a8-e9aff56d31ed/cssc202301223-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/490e65c0-c9da-43f0-85a8-e9aff56d31ed/cssc202301223-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7b9c79b3-5edb-4a6d-9042-3a0ec8f77b43/cssc202301223-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/490e65c0-c9da-43f0-85a8-e9aff56d31ed/cssc202301223-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance and kinetic analysis of PI/CNT-DIB. (a) CV curves at 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (b) EIS and its fitting diagram.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The self-discharge effect of the dual-ion battery is a critical concern that can′t be neglected, which is an important parameter for the practical application of the battery. A lower self-discharge rate can reduce the loss of specific discharge capacity so as to achieve excellent energy supply efficiency. And the self-discharge rate can be calculated via the following equation:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="cssc202301223-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/ff660356-71d2-4749-b632-81b036dfc1c4/cssc202301223-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>where <i>S</i> is the self-discharge rate, <i>C<sub>1</sub></i> is the specific discharge capacity without resting, <i>C<sub>2</sub></i> is the specific discharge capacity after shelving, and <i>T</i> is the shelving time.</p>
            
            <p>To ascertain the self-discharge process of the battery, the PI/CNT-DIB was charged to 3.8 V at a current density of 0.2 C and then discharged directly. The voltage at this point immediately drops from 3.5 V to 2.3 V with a specific discharge capacity of 119.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Then, a contrastive battery is rested for 72 h to survey the self-discharge. In Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0006">6</a>(a–d), according to the self-discharge effect of the battery, the voltage decreases to 2.3 V, and the specific discharge capacity decreases to 109.4 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after resting with an extremely low capacity loss of merely 9.9 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Therefore, the self-discharge rate of the battery after 72 h is calculated to be only 0.001 %/h, which reveals a rather low self-discharge rate. Thus, it is evident that the sodium ions have a strong bonding ability after inserted into the anode. Consequently, the self-discharge rate of the system is extremely low, suggesting that the battery delivers an excellent electrochemical performance.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/88de57c8-857d-4c98-870a-fac442201b48/cssc202301223-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/88de57c8-857d-4c98-870a-fac442201b48/cssc202301223-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/23eac182-3e4d-484b-9216-e1f33dfbc5fe/cssc202301223-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/88de57c8-857d-4c98-870a-fac442201b48/cssc202301223-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Self-discharge and fast charging performance test: (a–b) Unresting and (c–d) Resting for 72 h. (e) The first 10 cycles of charging at 30 C and discharging at 0.5 C and (f) Corresponding cyclic performance.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Throughout the entire industry market, the fast-charging performance of battery is one of the most expected features for customers. In this regard, fast-charging performance tests are carried out, in which the PI/CNT-DIB is charged rapidly at a current density of 30 C and slowly discharged at a current density of 0.5 C. According to the voltage-time graphs of the first ten cycles (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0006">6e</a>), it is surprising to discover that the battery is charged at average 45 s and discharged at average 22591 s (about 44 min). Additionally, the battery exhibits a good cycling performance (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0006">6f</a>), with a considerable specific discharge capacity of 88.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles as well as an excellent capacity retention of 100 % with a high CE close to 100 %. Through the above analysis, it can be concluded that the Na-DIBs system in this work can provide a relatively satisfactory performance for the fast-charging.</p>
            
            <p>The possible working mechanism of PI/CNT during the electrochemical reaction process are investigated based on the above electrochemical tests. PI/CNT-DIB undergoes a two-step enolization reaction during the charge and discharge process, and the reaction equations expressed in Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0007">7</a> are shown below.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/63b965f1-2ce7-4b1d-8410-db9267c99e6e/cssc202301223-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/63b965f1-2ce7-4b1d-8410-db9267c99e6e/cssc202301223-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1a192a74-ff03-4bd2-8314-42c74e766d98/cssc202301223-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/63b965f1-2ce7-4b1d-8410-db9267c99e6e/cssc202301223-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Investigations on the working mechanism based on PI/CNT anode. The working mechanisms of (a) PI/CNT-DIB and (b) PI/CNT electrod.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Anode:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="cssc202301223-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/3f2a0f6d-03b1-4bd9-abf2-e24aa1815d7b/cssc202301223-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Cathode:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="cssc202301223-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/5c3170d3-9eaa-41a8-911e-366fb41edf09/cssc202301223-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Overall:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="cssc202301223-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/93cd6aa8-e8d4-4c95-a0ce-569672ac698a/cssc202301223-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0004" title="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202301223:cssc202301223-math-0004" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>To further investigate and explore the redox mechanism of the Na-DIB, the cathode and anode are characterized by ex-situ XRD, Raman spectrum and FI-TR at different conditions during the charging and discharging process in the first cycle. The degree of graphitization and crystallinity of the graphite cathode shows an impact on its structural stability, electron conductivity and diffusion ability of the ions, which is in direct determining the (de-) intercalation dynamics of the anions through the electrode.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0048_R_d156382206e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew. Chem.">48</a></sup></span> According to Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0008">8b</a>, with the voltage increases from 2 V to 3.8 V in the charged state, the sharp 002 characteristic peak of graphite gradually shifts to the right, while the intensity of the characteristic peak also decreases. It is possible to be attributed to the insertion of TFSI<sup>−</sup> anion, leading to the widened layer spacing and reduced crystallinity of graphite.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0049_R_d156382206e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">49</a>, <a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0050_R_d156382206e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nat. Commun.">50</a></sup></span> As the voltage drops from 3.8 V to 0.01 V in the discharged state, the characteristic peaks of graphite are gradually shifting to the left, and the intensity is slightly restored, indicating the good reversibility of the insertion and de-insertion of the TFSI<sup>−</sup> anion. In addition, the reversible process was further demonstrated by Raman spectrum (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0008">8c</a>). The signal at 1330 cm<sup>−1</sup> is the D-peak of graphite, while the signal of 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup> refers to the G-peak of graphite.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202301223-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202301223-bib-0051_R_d156382206e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ChemElectroChem">51</a></sup></span> It can be observed that as the battery is charged from 2 V to 3.8 V and then fully discharged from 3.8 V to 0.01 V, the intensities of the D-, G- and 2D-peaks of graphite decreases first before returning to the initial state. Inspiringly, they still remain the initial state after 500 cycles, which can also suggest the reversible intercalation and deintercalation of the TFSI<sup>−</sup> anion into the graphite layer. Similarly, the PI/CNT anode undergoes the similar change. The reflection peak around 2θ = 12.4° is red-shifted with decreasing intensity following the increasing trend of the voltage (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0008">8e</a>). On the other hand, the characteristic peak of the C=O bond at around 1750 cm<sup>−1</sup> gradually becomes obvious and sharp from a broad peak when the voltage increases to 3.8 V (Figure <a href="#cssc202301223-fig-0008">8f</a>). This reflects that Na<sup>+</sup> cation is inserted into the PI/CNT anode and parts of the C=O functional groups are transformed into C−O−Na, resulting in the stabilization of the structure. The intensities and shifts of the characteristic peaks revert to the initial state along with the reduction of voltage, proving the high reversibility of the enolization process. The above test results confirm that both of the cathode and anode exhibit excellent structural stability.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202301223-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/06895292-5c1b-4a1e-9088-c6ba662a1da0/cssc202301223-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/06895292-5c1b-4a1e-9088-c6ba662a1da0/cssc202301223-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/61907e6e-146a-497c-8902-bbe791c56469/cssc202301223-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/06895292-5c1b-4a1e-9088-c6ba662a1da0/cssc202301223-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202301223-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202301223" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>XRD, FT-IR and Raman characterizations during charging and discharging. (a–c) XRD image and Raman spectrum of graphite cathode. (d–f) XRD image and FT-IR of PI/CNT anode.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To further investigate the structural stability of cathode and anode, both the pristine graphite cathode and the PI/CNT anode are charactered via the scanning electron microscopy, which in turn investigate and analyze the morphological and structural variations of the electrode before and after 500 cycles. Figure S4 shows that the pristine graphite cathode shows a relatively apparent lamellar structure, which is favorable for the insertion of TFSI<sup>−</sup> anion. On the other hand, the PI/CNT anode exhibits a rod-like structure. The morphologies of the graphite cathode and PI/CNT anode after 500 cycles are found to be almost invariant by SEM, and both of them are able to preserve their original morphology and structure, indicating that the cathode and anode materials deliver the outstanding structural stability, which is consistent well with the above-mentioned series of characterizations (XRD, Raman spectrum and FT-IR).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0005">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202301223-sec-0005-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully synthesized a type of polyimide composite (PI/CNT) by a facile condensation polymerization method. The material features the stable structure, higher storage energy and fast reaction kinetics of polyimides are favorable for improving specific capacity and stability. Successful complexation with carbon nanotubes also improves the conductivity of the electrode and increases the cation transfer rate. Owing to the unique conjugated structure and the synergia with CNT, the designed sodium-based dual-ion battery system formed by graphite as the cathode, PI/CNT as the anode, and Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI ionic liquid as the electrolyte exhibits a more satisfactory electrochemical performance. The PI/CNT-DIB shows a high specific discharge capacity of 119.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C and achieves a long-term cycling stability of 82.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 10 C, with both the capacity retention and coulombic efficiency close to 100 %. Even at 30 C, the battery still keeps a considerable specific discharge capacity. The self-discharge rate is extremely low (0.001 %/h). In addition, a two-step redox reaction is confirmed using CV test, and the reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and desertion in the PI/CNT anode is further demonstrated by XRD, FT-IR and Raman characterizations. Thus, this work effectively designs a green and sustainable material with considerable potential for energy storage devices.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202301223-sec-0006-title"> Experimental Detail</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202301223-sec-0007-title"> Materials</h3>
            
            <p>Part of the materials used in this experiment, such as urea, 3,4,9,10-Perylenetetracarboxylic dianhydride (PTCDA), N-Methylpyrrolidone (NMP), short multi-walled carbon nanotubeanhydrous ethanol were purchased from Aladdin Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd. Acetylene black, poly (vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) and aluminum foil were purchased from Arkema. 1-butyl-1-methylpyrrolidinium bis (trifluoromethanesulfonyl) imide salt (Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI) ionic liquid was purchased from Lanzhou Institute of Chemical Physics. Sodium bis-(trifluoromethanesulfonimide) (NaTFSI) was purchased from Macklin. All materials were used directly without any purification.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202301223-sec-0008-title"> Preparation of PI and PI/CNT</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0009">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc202301223-sec-0009-title"> Synthesis of PI</h4>
               
               <p>First, 5 mmol of PTCDA was weighed, dissolved in 100 ml of NMP solution, stirred for 5 min, and then a slightly excessive amount of urea was added and stirred until dissolved. The mixture was refluxed at 180 °C for 8 h. Then, the mixture was washed three times with deionized water and anhydrous ethanol by high-speed centrifugation, and the obtained precipitate was dried in a vacuum oven at 100 °C overnight. At the end, the material was deposited in a tube furnace (Ar, 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>) and heated at 350 °C for 8 h.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0010">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc202301223-sec-0010-title"> Synthesis of PI/CNT</h4>
               
               <p>Specifically, short multi-walled carbon nanotubes (10 mg) were dispersed in NMP solution and sonicated in an ultrasonic bath at 40 kHz for 30 min. Afterwards, a weighed amount of PTCDA (5 mmol) was stirred by adding it to the above dispersion, and then a slight excess of urea was mixed until dissolution. The following steps were performed in the same way as for the preparation of PI.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0011">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202301223-sec-0011-title"> Electrodes preparation and cell assembly</h3>
            
            <p>The preparation of the anode was performed as follows: the ground sample powder, acetylene black and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) were mixed in a mass ratio of 7 : 2 : 1 and stirred overnight with NMP as solvent. The obtained slurry was coated uniformly at a thickness of 200 μm on a pure aluminum foil collector and then transferred to a vacuum drying oven for overnight drying at 70 °C. The fabricated electrode sheets were sliced by a slicer with a diameter of 14 mm to reach uniformly sized round electrode sheets. The average loading of the anode material was calculated to be 2.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The preparation technology of graphite cathode is similar to that of the anode. Graphite powder: acetylene black: polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF)=8 : 1 : 1 mass ratio is mixed and coated with a thickness of 200 μm. The average loading of the cathode material was 2.6 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>The electrolyte used was 100 μL 1 M NaTFSI (Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI). The full cell was assembled using a CR2025 type battery case, using glass fibers as separators. All cells were assembled in an argon-filled glove box ([O<sub>2</sub>] &lt;0.01 ppm, [H<sub>2</sub>O] &lt;0.01 ppm). After standing 12 h, the cells tested on the Neware CT-4008 battery test system (Shenzhen, China). Gamry electrochemical workstation was adopted for cyclic voltammetry (CV) testing. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were also tested by Gamry electrochemical workstation with an amplitude of 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and a frequency range of 0.01 Hz to 10<sup>5</sup> Hz.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0012">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202301223-sec-0012-title"> Characterization of Material and Electrode</h3>
            
            <p>The characterization tests on the synthesized samples require equipment such as field emission scanning electron microscope (Zeiss, Germany), fourier transform infrared spectrometer (Thermo Fisher, USA), multi-layer auto-feed X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku, Japan), laser confocal micro raman spectrometer (Horiba Jobin Yvon, French), thermogravimetric Analysis (NETZZCH, Germany) and N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms (Microparticle ASAP2460 analyzer, USA).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202301223-sec-0013">
